This is a common pain point when working with LLM systems that require programmatic input.\n\n## Potential Solutions\n\n### 1. Raw Strings\n\nFor simpler cases, Python's raw strings can help:\n```python\ncode_snippet = r\"\"\"def example():\n print(\"This won't cause problems with quotes\")\n \"\"\"\n```\n\nHowever, raw strings still have limitations with triple quotes inside them.\n\n### 2. Dedicated Text Files\n\nA cleaner approach is to store complex content in separate files:\n```python\ndef read_prompt(file_path):\n with open(file_path, \"r\") as f:\n return f.read()\n \ncode_prompt = read_prompt(\"./prompts/code_sample.py\")\nchat.commit(code_prompt)\n```\n\nThis has the added benefit of syntax highlighting in your editor and version control tracking.\n\n### 3. Multiline Strings with Escape Sequences\n\nUsing explicit escaping:\n```python\ncode_snippet = (\n \"def example():\\n\"\n \" print(\\\"Hello world\\\")\\n\"\n \" '''\\n\"\n \" This is a docstring\\n\"\n \" '''\"\n)\n```\n\n### 4. Template System\n\nYou could implement a simple template system:\n```python\nfrom string import Template\n\ntemplate = Template(read_prompt(\"./prompts/template.txt\"))\nfilled_prompt = template.substitute(\n code_example=read_prompt(\"./prompts/code_example.py\"),\n data_sample=read_prompt(\"./prompts/data.json\")\n)\n```\n\n### 5. Add a Helper Method to Chat\n\nYou could add a method to the `Chat` class that handles this automatically:\n\n```python\ndef commit_from_file(self, file_path, role=\"user\", **kwargs):\n \"\"\"Commit a message from a file to avoid string escaping issues\"\"\"\n with open(file_path, \"r\") as f:\n content = f.read()\n return self.commit(content, role=role, **kwargs)\n```\n\nThen use it as:\n```python\nchat.commit_from_file(\"./prompts/complex_code.py\")\n```\n\n### 6. Heredoc-style Approach\n\nSome libraries like `textwrap.dedent` can help maintain formatting:\n```python\nimport textwrap\n\ncode_snippet = textwrap.dedent(\"\"\"\n def example():\n print(\"Hello world\")\n '''\n This is a docstring\n '''\n\"\"\").strip()\n```\n\n## Recommendation\n\nI'd recommend implementing a combination of approaches 2 and 5 - add a helper method to the `Chat` class that can read content from files. This would provide the most flexibility and avoid string escaping issues entirely.\n\nFor your existing code base, you could even extend this with a simple template system to allow for variable substitution in prompts stored as files, giving you the best of both worlds.", "role": "assistant", "tool_calls": null, "function_call": null}, "children": {"master": "7fed9b3c", "vim": "057e186c"}, "parent_id": "dc7df04f", "home_branch": "master"}, "7fed9b3c": {"id": "7fed9b3c", "message": {"role": "user", "content": "Can you tell me more about Heredoc/textwrap.dedent etc? I'm not sure I understand how they work. What are their advantages and disadvantages?"}, "children": {"master": "ef9cb79c"}, "parent_id": "f5eb2cb2", "home_branch": "master"}, "ef9cb79c": {"id": "ef9cb79c", "message": {"content": "# Understanding Heredoc-Style Approaches in Python\n\n## What is a \"Heredoc\"?\n\nA \"heredoc\" (short for \"here document\") is a way to define a multi-line string literal in programming languages, originally from Unix shell scripting. While Python doesn't have a native heredoc syntax like some languages (e.g., Perl, PHP, Bash), it offers alternatives like triple-quoted strings and the `textwrap.dedent()` function.\n\n## Python's `textwrap.dedent()`\n\n### How It Works\n\n`textwrap.dedent()` removes common leading whitespace from each line of a text string, which helps maintain proper indentation in your code while creating properly formatted multi-line strings.\n\n```python\nimport textwrap\n\ncode_example = textwrap.dedent(\"\"\"\n def hello_world():\n print(\"Hello, world!\")\n '''\n This is a docstring with \"quotes\"\n and triple quotes '''\n '''\n \n for i in range(10):\n # Indented code\n print(i)\n \"\"\").strip() # strip() removes leading/trailing newlines\n\nprint(code_example)\n```\n\nWhen executed, this code produces:\n\n```\ndef hello_world():\n print(\"Hello, world!\")\n '''\n This is a docstring with \"quotes\"\n and triple quotes '''\n '''\n \n for i in range(10):\n # Indented code\n print(i)\n```\n\n### How Dedentation Works\n\n1. `textwrap.dedent()` examines all lines in the string\n2. It identifies the minimum amount of leading whitespace common to all non-blank lines\n3. It removes exactly that amount of whitespace from the beginning of all lines\n\n### Advantages\n\n1. **Code Readability**: Your code maintains proper indentation and is more readable\n2. **No Manual Escaping**: No need to manually escape quotes or special characters\n3. **Preserves Format**: Newlines, internal indentation, and whitespace are preserved exactly\n4. **Prevents Indentation Errors**: The indentation in the final string is exactly what you see in the code\n\n### Disadvantages\n\n1. **Not True Heredoc**: It's not a true heredoc like in other languages (where content is treated as literal text)\n2. **Can't Handle Variable Indentation**: If some lines have less indentation than others, those lines will determine the common indentation\n3. **Limited Escaping Control**: You still need to be careful with backslashes in strings\n4. **Requires Import**: Needs to import the `textwrap` module\n\n## Alternative: Triple-Quoted Strings\n\nTriple-quoted strings in Python (`\"\"\"...\"\"\"` or `'''...'''`) are Python's native multi-line string approach.\n\n```python\ncode_example = \"\"\"\ndef hello_world():\n print(\"Hello, world!\")\n '''\n This is a docstring with quotes\n and triple quotes '''\n '''\n\"\"\"\n```\n\n### Advantages of Triple-Quoted Strings\n\n1. **Built-in**: No additional imports needed\n2. **Simple**: Easy to understand and use\n\n### Disadvantages of Triple-Quoted Strings\n\n1. **Indentation Issues**: Leading whitespace is preserved, which can lead to undesired indentation\n2. **Limited Quote Handling**: Difficulty with content containing triple quotes\n3. **Visual Inconsistency**: Often breaks your code's visual indentation\n\n## When to Use `textwrap.dedent()`\n\n`textwrap.dedent()` is particularly useful when:\n\n1. You're including code examples, formatted text, or other content where whitespace matters\n2. The indentation of your Python code doesn't match the indentation you want in your string\n3. You need a clean, readable way to define multi-line strings that might contain quotes\n\n## Example in Your Chat System\n\nFor your chat system, you could implement a helper that uses `textwrap.dedent()`:\n\n```python\nimport textwrap\n\ndef commit_code(self, code_snippet, language=\"python\", comment=None):\n \"\"\"Commit a code snippet with proper formatting.\"\"\"\n code = textwrap.dedent(code_snippet).strip()\n message = f\"{comment}\\n\\n```{language}\\n{code}\\n```\" if comment else f\"```{language}\\n{code}\\n```\"\n return self.commit(message)\n\n# Usage:\nchat.commit_code(\"\"\"\n def example():\n print(\"Hello world\")\n '''\n This is a docstring\n '''\n # This indentation is preserved\n for i in range(10):\n print(i)\n \"\"\", \n comment=\"Here's a Python function that prints numbers:\")\n```\n\nThis approach combines the best of both worlds: clean, visually consistent code in your Python files, and properly formatted text in your LLM prompts.", "role": "assistant", "tool_calls": null, "function_call": null}, "children": {"master": null}, "parent_id": "7fed9b3c", "home_branch": "master"}, "057e186c": {"id": "057e186c", "message": {"role": "user", "content": "Maybe a better solution is: when the user gives message \"^N\", it creates and opens a temporary text file where the user can write their message, save and exit. Here's how you could implement this feature in your `Chat` class:\n\n```python\nimport os\nimport tempfile\nimport subprocess\nfrom pathlib import Path\n\ndef commit_with_editor(self, role=\"user\", initial_content=\"\", editor=None, **kwargs):\n \"\"\"\n Open a text editor for the user to compose their message.\n \n When the user types \"^N\" as their message, this method will be called.\n It opens a temporary file in the user's preferred editor, allowing them\n to write their message with proper syntax highlighting and without\n string escaping issues.\n \n Args:\n role: The role for the message (user, assistant, system)\n initial_content: Optional text to pre-populate the editor\n editor: Optional editor command. When trying to launch an external editor from a Jupyter notebook cell, it can't properly interact with the standard input, which is why you're seeing the \"Standard input is not a terminal\" error.\n\nHere's a better approach specifically designed to work within Jupyter notebooks:\n\n```python\ndef commit_with_editor(self, role=\"user\", initial_content=\"\", **kwargs):\n \"\"\"\n Open a text editor for the user to compose their message, compatible with Jupyter notebooks.\n \n Args:\n role: The role for the message (user, assistant, system)\n initial_content: Optional text to pre-populate the editor\n **kwargs: Additional arguments to pass to commit()\n \n Returns:\n Result of commit() with the edited content\n \"\"\"\n import ipywidgets as widgets\n from IPython.display import display\n \n # Create a multi-line text area widget\n text_area = widgets.Textarea(\n value=initial_content,\n placeholder='Type your message here...',\n description='Message:',\n disabled=False,\n layout=widgets.Layout(width='100%', height='300px')\n )\n \n # Create a button to submit the text\n button = widgets.Button(\n description='Commit Message',\n button_style='success',\n tooltip='Click to commit this message'\n )\n \n output = widgets.Output()\n \n # Store the result in a mutable container\n result_container = {'committed': False}\n \n def on_button_click(b):\n content = text_area.value\n with output:\n preview = content[:200] + \"...\" if len(content) > 200 else content\n print(f\"Committing message ({len(content)} characters):\\n{preview}\")\n \n # Commit the message\n self.commit(message=content, role=role, **kwargs)\n result_container['committed'] = True\n \n button.on_click(on_button_click)\n \n # Display the widget and button\n display(text_area, button, output)\n \n # Note: This function will return immediately, and the commit will happen\n # asynchronously when the user clicks the button\n return \"Editor opened. Click 'Commit Message' when done.\"\n```\n\n### Alternative approach using a temporary file\n\nIf you prefer not to use widgets or if they're not available, here's a simpler approach that should work in Jupyter:\n\n```python\ndef commit_with_editor(self, role=\"user\", **kwargs):\n \"\"\"\n Create a temporary file and provide instructions for the user to edit it\n and then upload it back. However, when you modify the variable through the module reference (chit.FORCE), you're only modifying your local reference to that variable, not the original variable in the module's namespace.\n\nTo make this work as you expect, you have a few options:\n\n1. Use a configuration class or object:\n```python\n# In your module (chit.py)\nclass Config:\n VERBOSE = True\n FORCE = False\n AUTOSAVE = True\n EDITOR = \"code\"\n\nconfig = Config()\n\ndef cconfirm(prompt: str) -> bool:\n print(config.FORCE)\n if not config.FORCE:\n response = input(f\"{prompt} (y/n) \")\n return response.lower() == \"y\"\n return True\n```\n\nThen you can modify it like:\n```python\nimport chit\nchit.config.FORCE = True\n```\n\n2. Use a module-level dictionary:\n```python\n# In your module (chit.py)\nsettings = {\n 'VERBOSE': True,\n 'FORCE': False,\n 'AUTOSAVE': True,\n 'EDITOR': \"code\"\n}\n\ndef cconfirm(prompt: str) -> bool:\n print(settings['FORCE'])\n if not settings['FORCE']:\n response = input(f\"{prompt} (y/n) \")\n return response.lower() == \"y\"\n return True\n```\n\nThen you can modify it like:\n```python\nimport chit\nchit.settings['FORCE'] = True\n```\n\n3. Create setter functions:\n```python\n# In your module (chit.py)\nVERBOSE = True\nFORCE = False\nAUTOSAVE = True\nEDITOR = \"code\"\n\ndef set_force(value: bool):\n global FORCE\n FORCE = value\n\ndef cconfirm(prompt: str) -> bool:\n print(FORCE)\n if not FORCE:\n response = input(f\"{prompt} (y/n) \")\n return response.lower() == \"y\"\n return True\n```\n\nThen you can modify it like:\n```python\nimport chit\nchit.set_force(True)\n```\n\nThe first two options (using a class or dictionary) are generally considered better practice as they make it clearer that these are configuration values that might change during runtime. I'm not sure I understand how they work. What are their advantages and disadvantages?\"}, \"children\": {\"master\": \"ef9cb79c\"}, \"parent_id\": \"f5eb2cb2\", \"home_branch\": \"master\"}, \"ef9cb79c\": {\"id\": \"ef9cb79c\", \"message\": {\"content\": \"# Understanding Heredoc-Style Approaches in Python\\n\\n## What is a \\\"Heredoc\\\"?\\n\\nA \\\"heredoc\\\" (short for \\\"here document\\\") is a way to define a multi-line string literal in programming languages, originally from Unix shell scripting. While Python doesn't have a native heredoc syntax like some languages (e.g., Perl, PHP, Bash), it offers alternatives like triple-quoted strings and the `textwrap.dedent()` function.\\n\\n## Python's `textwrap.dedent()`\\n\\n### How It Works\\n\\n`textwrap.dedent()` removes common leading whitespace from each line of a text string, which helps maintain proper indentation in your code while creating properly formatted multi-line strings.\\n\\n```python\\nimport textwrap\\n\\ncode_example = textwrap.dedent(\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n def hello_world():\\n print(\\\"Hello, world!\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring with \\\"quotes\\\"\\n and triple quotes '''\\n '''\\n \\n for i in range(10):\\n # Indented code\\n print(i)\\n \\\"\\\"\\\").strip() # strip() removes leading/trailing newlines\\n\\nprint(code_example)\\n```\\n\\nWhen executed, this code produces:\\n\\n```\\ndef hello_world():\\n print(\\\"Hello, world!\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring with \\\"quotes\\\"\\n and triple quotes '''\\n '''\\n \\n for i in range(10):\\n # Indented code\\n print(i)\\n```\\n\\n### How Dedentation Works\\n\\n1. `textwrap.dedent()` examines all lines in the string\\n2. It identifies the minimum amount of leading whitespace common to all non-blank lines\\n3. It removes exactly that amount of whitespace from the beginning of all lines\\n\\n### Advantages\\n\\n1. **Code Readability**: Your code maintains proper indentation and is more readable\\n2. **No Manual Escaping**: No need to manually escape quotes or special characters\\n3. **Preserves Format**: Newlines, internal indentation, and whitespace are preserved exactly\\n4. **Prevents Indentation Errors**: The indentation in the final string is exactly what you see in the code\\n\\n### Disadvantages\\n\\n1. **Not True Heredoc**: It's not a true heredoc like in other languages (where content is treated as literal text)\\n2. **Can't Handle Variable Indentation**: If some lines have less indentation than others, those lines will determine the common indentation\\n3. **Limited Escaping Control**: You still need to be careful with backslashes in strings\\n4. **Requires Import**: Needs to import the `textwrap` module\\n\\n## Alternative: Triple-Quoted Strings\\n\\nTriple-quoted strings in Python (`\\\"\\\"\\\"...\\\"\\\"\\\"` or `'''...'''`) are Python's native multi-line string approach.\\n\\n```python\\ncode_example = \\\"\\\"\\\"\\ndef hello_world():\\n print(\\\"Hello, world!\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring with quotes\\n and triple quotes '''\\n '''\\n\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n```\\n\\n### Advantages of Triple-Quoted Strings\\n\\n1. **Built-in**: No additional imports needed\\n2. **Simple**: Easy to understand and use\\n\\n### Disadvantages of Triple-Quoted Strings\\n\\n1. **Indentation Issues**: Leading whitespace is preserved, which can lead to undesired indentation\\n2. **Limited Quote Handling**: Difficulty with content containing triple quotes\\n3. **Visual Inconsistency**: Often breaks your code's visual indentation\\n\\n## When to Use `textwrap.dedent()`\\n\\n`textwrap.dedent()` is particularly useful when:\\n\\n1. You're including code examples, formatted text, or other content where whitespace matters\\n2. The indentation of your Python code doesn't match the indentation you want in your string\\n3. You need a clean, readable way to define multi-line strings that might contain quotes\\n\\n## Example in Your Chat System\\n\\nFor your chat system, you could implement a helper that uses `textwrap.dedent()`:\\n\\n```python\\nimport textwrap\\n\\ndef commit_code(self, code_snippet, language=\\\"python\\\", comment=None):\\n \\\"\\\"\\\"Commit a code snippet with proper formatting.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n code = textwrap.dedent(code_snippet).strip()\\n message = f\\\"{comment}\\\\n\\\\n```{language}\\\\n{code}\\\\n```\\\" if comment else f\\\"```{language}\\\\n{code}\\\\n```\\\"\\n return self.commit(message)\\n\\n# Usage:\\nchat.commit_code(\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n def example():\\n print(\\\"Hello world\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring\\n '''\\n # This indentation is preserved\\n for i in range(10):\\n print(i)\\n \\\"\\\"\\\", \\n comment=\\\"Here's a Python function that prints numbers:\\\")\\n```\\n\\nThis approach combines the best of both worlds: clean, visually consistent code in your Python files, and properly formatted text in your LLM prompts.\", \"role\": \"assistant\", \"tool_calls\": null, \"function_call\": null}, \"children\": {\"master\": null}, \"parent_id\": \"7fed9b3c\", \"home_branch\": \"master\"}, \"057e186c\": {\"id\": \"057e186c\", \"message\": {\"role\": \"user\", \"content\": \"Maybe a better solution is: when the user gives message \\\"^N\\\", it creates and opens a temporary text file where the user can write their message, save and exit. While Python doesn't have a native heredoc syntax like some languages (e.g., Perl, PHP, Bash), it offers alternatives like triple-quoted strings and the `textwrap.dedent()` function.\\n\\n## Python's `textwrap.dedent()`\\n\\n### How It Works\\n\\n`textwrap.dedent()` removes common leading whitespace from each line of a text string, which helps maintain proper indentation in your code while creating properly formatted multi-line strings.\\n\\n```python\\nimport textwrap\\n\\ncode_example = textwrap.dedent(\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n def hello_world():\\n print(\\\"Hello, world!\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring with \\\"quotes\\\"\\n and triple quotes '''\\n '''\\n \\n for i in range(10):\\n # Indented code\\n print(i)\\n \\\"\\\"\\\").strip() # strip() removes leading/trailing newlines\\n\\nprint(code_example)\\n```\\n\\nWhen executed, this code produces:\\n\\n```\\ndef hello_world():\\n print(\\\"Hello, world!\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring with \\\"quotes\\\"\\n and triple quotes '''\\n '''\\n \\n for i in range(10):\\n # Indented code\\n print(i)\\n```\\n\\n### How Dedentation Works\\n\\n1. `textwrap.dedent()` examines all lines in the string\\n2. It identifies the minimum amount of leading whitespace common to all non-blank lines\\n3. It removes exactly that amount of whitespace from the beginning of all lines\\n\\n### Advantages\\n\\n1. **Code Readability**: Your code maintains proper indentation and is more readable\\n2. **No Manual Escaping**: No need to manually escape quotes or special characters\\n3. **Preserves Format**: Newlines, internal indentation, and whitespace are preserved exactly\\n4. **Prevents Indentation Errors**: The indentation in the final string is exactly what you see in the code\\n\\n### Disadvantages\\n\\n1. **Not True Heredoc**: It's not a true heredoc like in other languages (where content is treated as literal text)\\n2. **Can't Handle Variable Indentation**: If some lines have less indentation than others, those lines will determine the common indentation\\n3. **Limited Escaping Control**: You still need to be careful with backslashes in strings\\n4. **Requires Import**: Needs to import the `textwrap` module\\n\\n## Alternative: Triple-Quoted Strings\\n\\nTriple-quoted strings in Python (`\\\"\\\"\\\"...\\\"\\\"\\\"` or `'''...'''`) are Python's native multi-line string approach.\\n\\n```python\\ncode_example = \\\"\\\"\\\"\\ndef hello_world():\\n print(\\\"Hello, world!\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring with quotes\\n and triple quotes '''\\n '''\\n\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n```\\n\\n### Advantages of Triple-Quoted Strings\\n\\n1. **Built-in**: No additional imports needed\\n2. **Simple**: Easy to understand and use\\n\\n### Disadvantages of Triple-Quoted Strings\\n\\n1. **Indentation Issues**: Leading whitespace is preserved, which can lead to undesired indentation\\n2. **Limited Quote Handling**: Difficulty with content containing triple quotes\\n3. **Visual Inconsistency**: Often breaks your code's visual indentation\\n\\n## When to Use `textwrap.dedent()`\\n\\n`textwrap.dedent()` is particularly useful when:\\n\\n1. You're including code examples, formatted text, or other content where whitespace matters\\n2. The indentation of your Python code doesn't match the indentation you want in your string\\n3. You need a clean, readable way to define multi-line strings that might contain quotes\\n\\n## Example in Your Chat System\\n\\nFor your chat system, you could implement a helper that uses `textwrap.dedent()`:\\n\\n```python\\nimport textwrap\\n\\ndef commit_code(self, code_snippet, language=\\\"python\\\", comment=None):\\n \\\"\\\"\\\"Commit a code snippet with proper formatting.\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n code = textwrap.dedent(code_snippet).strip()\\n message = f\\\"{comment}\\\\n\\\\n```{language}\\\\n{code}\\\\n```\\\" if comment else f\\\"```{language}\\\\n{code}\\\\n```\\\"\\n return self.commit(message)\\n\\n# Usage:\\nchat.commit_code(\\\"\\\"\\\"\\n def example():\\n print(\\\"Hello world\\\")\\n '''\\n This is a docstring\\n '''\\n # This indentation is preserved\\n for i in range(10):\\n print(i)\\n \\\"\\\"\\\", \\n comment=\\\"Here's a Python function that prints numbers:\\\")\\n```\\n\\nThis approach combines the best of both worlds: clean, visually consistent code in your Python files, and properly formatted text in your LLM prompts.\", \"role\": \"assistant\", \"tool_calls\": null, \"function_call\": null}, \"children\": {\"master\": null}, \"parent_id\": \"7fed9b3c\", \"home_branch\": \"master\"}, \"057e186c\": {\"id\": \"057e186c\", \"message\": {\"role\": \"user\", \"content\": \"Maybe a better solution is: when the user gives message \\\"^N\\\", it creates and opens a temporary text file where the user can write their message, save and exit.
